ArXiv API¶

The ArXiV API allows programmatic access to the arXiv’s e-print content and metadata. “The goal of the interface is to facilitate new and creative use of the the vast body of material on the arXiv by providing a low barrier to entry for application developers.” https://arxiv.org/help/api

The API’s user manual (https://arxiv.org/help/api/user-manual) provides helpful documentation for using the API and retrieving article metadata.

Our examples below will introduce you to the basics of querying the ArXiV API.

Install Packages¶

import urllib
import arxiv
import requests
import json
import csv
import pandas as pd
from collections import Counter, defaultdict
import numpy as np # for array manipulation
import matplotlib.pyplot as plt # for data visualization
%matplotlib inline 
import datetime

Query the API¶

Perform a simple query for graphene. We’ll limit results to 100. Take a look at the information you get back and how it is structured.

graphene = arxiv.query(query="graphene", max_results=100)
graphene

In present work, we propose a heterostructure of graphene\nencapsulated by hydrogenated graphene which could be a promising candidate for\na variety of device applications. We have carried out DFT calculations on this\nsystem to check its feasibility to be a versatile material. We found that\nelectronic states of multilayer pristine graphene, especially Dirac cone, an\nimportant feature to host QSHE, can be preserved by sandwiching it by fully\nhydrogenated graphene. Interference of electronic states of hydrogenated\ngraphene was insignificant with those of graphene. States of graphene were also\nfound to be stable upon application of electric field up to 2.5V/nm. For device\napplications, multilayer graphene or its heterostructures are required to be\ndeposited on a substrate, which interacts with system opening up a gap at Dirac\ncone making it less suitable for QSHE applications and hydrogenated graphene\ncan prevent it. Magnetization in these hydrogenated graphene sandwiched\ngraphene may be induced by creating vacancies or distortions in hydrogenated\ngraphene, which was found to have minimal effect on graphenes electronic\nstates, thus providing an additional degree of manipulation. We also performed\na set of calculations to explore its applicability for detecting some\nmolecules. Our results on trilayer graphene encapsulated by hydrogenated\ngraphene indicate that all these observations can be generalized to systems\nwith a larger number of graphene layers, indicating that multilayer graphene\nsandwiched between two hydrogenated graphene is a versatile material that can\nbe used in QSHE, sensor devices, etc.',

The ultra-thin profile of graphene, however, poses\nsignificant challenge to direct measurement of its adhesion property using\nconventional approaches. We show that there is a strong correlation between the\nmorphology of graphene on a compliant substrate with patterned surface and the\ngraphene-substrate adhesion. The sharp transition between these two\ntypes of graphene morphology occurs at a critical adhesion between the graphene\nand the compliant substrate material. These results potentially open up a\nfeasible pathway to measuring the adhesion property of graphene.',

  'summary': 'As a possible way of modifying the intrinsic properties of graphene we study\nthe doping of graphene by embedded boron clusters with density functional\ntheory. Cluster doping is technologically relevant as the cluster implantation\ntechnique can be readily applied to graphene. We find that B7 clusters embedded\ninto graphene and graphene nanoribbons are structurally stable and locally\nmetallize the system. This is done both by the reduction of the Fermi energy\nand by the introduction of boron states near the Fermi level. A linear chain of\nboron clusters forms a metallic "wire" inside the graphene matrix. In a zigzag\nedge graphene nanoribbon the cluster-related states tend to hybridize with the\nedge and bulk states. The magnetism in boron doped graphene systems is\ngenerally very weak. The presence of boron clusters weakens the edge magnetism\nin zigzag edge graphene nanoribbon, rather than making the system appropriate\nfor spintronics. Thus the doping of graphene with the cluster implantation\ntechnique might be a viable technique to locally metallize graphene without\ndestroying its attractive bulk properties.',

In a zigzag\nedge graphene nanoribbon the cluster-related states tend to hybridize with the\nedge and bulk states. The magnetism in boron doped graphene systems is\ngenerally very weak. The presence of boron clusters weakens the edge magnetism\nin zigzag edge graphene nanoribbon, rather than making the system appropriate\nfor spintronics. Thus the doping of graphene with the cluster implantation\ntechnique might be a viable technique to locally metallize graphene without\ndestroying its attractive bulk properties.'},

  'summary': 'Pentacene has been used widely in organic devices, and the interface\nstructure between pentacene and a substrate is known to significantly influence\ndevice performances. Here we demonstrate that molecular ordering of pentacene\non graphene depends on the interaction between graphene and its underlying SiC\nsubstrate. The adsorption of pentacene molecules on zero-layer and single-layer\ngraphene, which were grown on a Sifaced 6H-SiC(0001) wafer, was studied using\nscanning tunneling microscopy (STM). Pentacene molecules form a quasi-amorphous\nlayer on zero-layer graphene which interacts strongly with the underlying SiC\nsubstrate. In contrast, they form a uniformly ordered layer on the single-layer\ngraphene having a weak graphene-SiC interaction. Furthermore, we could change\nthe configuration of pentacene molecules on the singlelayer graphene by using\nSTM tips. The results suggest that the molecular ordering of pentacene on\ngraphene and the pentacene/graphene interface structure can be controlled by a\ngraphene-substrate interaction.',

  'summary': 'In this Letter, using quartz crystal microbalance (QCM), we experimentally\ndetermined the mass density of graphene grown by chemical vapor deposition\nmethod. We developed a transfer printing technique to integrate large area\nsingle-layer graphene on QCM. By monitoring the resonant frequency of an\noscillating quartz crystal loaded with graphene, we were able to measure the\nmass density of graphene as ~118 ng/cm2, which is significantly larger than the\nideal graphene (~76 ng/cm2) mainly due to the presence of wrinkles and\norganic/inorganic residues on graphene sheets. High sensitivity of quartz\ncrystal resonator allowed us to determine the number of graphene layers in\nsamples. (The technique is very sensitive and able to determine the number of\ngraphene layers in a particular sample.) Besides, we extended our technique to\nprobe interfacial mass variation during adsorption of biomolecules on graphene\nsurface, and plasma-assisted oxidation of graphene. (Besides, we extended our\ntechnique to probe interfacial mass variation during adsorption of biomolecules\non graphene surface, and plasma-assisted oxidation of graphene.)',

  'title_detail': {'type': 'text/plain',

   'value': 'Contrasting diffusion behaviors of O and F atoms on graphene and within\n  bilayer graphene'},
  'summary': 'The chemical modification of graphene with adatoms is of importance for\nnanoelectronics applications. Based on first-principles density-functional\ntheory calculations with including van der Waals interactions, we present a\ncomparative study of the diffusion characteristics of oxygen (O) and fluorine\n(F) atoms both on graphene and between the layers of bilayer graphene. We find\nthat O and F atoms have lower binding energies between the layers of bilayer\ngraphene compared to on graphene. Interestingly, the calculated diffusion\nbarrier for the O atom slightly increases from 0.81 eV on graphene to 0.85 eV\nwithin bilayer graphene, while that for the F atom significantly decreases from\n0.30 eV on graphene to 0.18 eV within bilayer graphene. Such contrasting\nbehaviors of the O and F diffusions within bilayer graphene can be traced to\ntheir different bonding natures: i.e., the O adatom that has a strongly\ncovalent C$-$O$-$C bonding on the bridge site of the C$-$C bond diffuses on one\ngraphene layer with a slight interference of the other layer, while the F\nadatom that has a weakly ionic F$-$C bonding on top of a C atom easily diffuses\nby hopping between two graphene layers with accepting more electron charges\nfrom the two layers. The present findings have important implications for\nunderstanding the diffusion processes of F and O atoms on graphene and within\nbilayer graphene.',

  'summary': 'Graphene plasmonics has become a highlighted research area due to the\noutstanding properties of deep-subwavelength plasmon excitation, long\nrelaxation time, and electro-optical tunability. Although the giant\nconductivity of a graphene layer enables the low-dimensional confinement of\nlight, the atomic scale of the layer thickness is severely mismatched with\noptical mode sizes, which impedes the efficient tuning of graphene plasmon\nmodes from the degraded light-graphene overlap. Inspired by gap plasmon modes\nin noble metals, here we propose low-dimensional graphene gap plasmon waves for\nlarge light-graphene overlap factor. We show that gap plasmon waves exhibit\nsuperior in-plane and out-of-plane field concentrations on graphene compared to\nthose of edge or wire-like graphene plasmons. By adjusting the chemical\nproperty of the graphene layer, efficient and linear modulation of graphene gap\nplasmon modes is also achieved. Our results provide potential opportunities to\nlow-dimensional graphene plasmonic devices with strong tunability.',

  'summary': 'We investigate the structural damage of graphene underlying dielectrics (HfO2\nand Al2O3) by remote plasma-enhanced atomic layer deposition (PE-ALD).\nDielectric film is grown on bilayer graphene without inducing significant\ndamage to the bottom graphene layer. Based on Raman spectra, we demonstrate\nthat the bottom graphene layer has the salient features of single layer\ngraphene. During the initial half-cycle PE-ALD, the upper graphene layer reacts\nwith the metal precursor, forming uniform nucleation islands or an active\nmetallic carbide layer. After monolayer dielectric coverage, the bottom\ngraphene layer has additional protection. The upper graphene layer serves as a\nsacrificial layer, which not only promotes the adhesion of dielectric on\ngraphene, but also protects the lattice symmetry of the bottom graphene layer.\nOur results indicate that bilayer graphene allows for controlling/limiting the\ndegree of defect during the ALD of dielectrics and could be a good starting\nmaterial for building filed effect transistors and sensing devices.',

Scanning-Raman-Microscopy (SRM) is\nused to characterize films of graphene derived from flakes of graphene oxide\nwith an almost intact carbon framework (ai-GO). The defect density of these\nflakes is visualized in detail by analyzing the intensity and full-width at\nhalf-maximum of the most pronounced Raman peaks. In addition, we superimpose\nthe SRM results with AFM images and correlate the spectroscopic results with\nthe morphology. Furthermore, we use SRM technique to display the amount of\ndefects in a film of graphene. Thus, an area of 250 x 250 {\\my}m2 of graphene\nis probed with a step-size increment of 1 {\\mu}m. We are able to visualize the\nposition of graphene flakes, edges and the substrate.   'summary': 'Graphene (G) is a two-dimensional material with exceptional sensing\nproperties. In general, graphene gas sensors are produced in field effect\ntransistor configuration on several substrates. The role of the substrates on\nthe sensor characteristics has not yet been entirely established. To provide\nfurther insight on the interaction between ammonia molecules (NH3) and graphene\ndevices, we report experimental and theoretical studies of NH3 graphene sensors\nwith graphene supported on three substrates: SiO2, talc and hexagonal boron\nnitride (hBN). Our results indicate that the charge transfer from NH3 to\ngraphene depends not only on extrinsic parameters like temperature and gas\nconcentration, but also on the average distance between the graphene sheet and\nthe substrate. We find that the average distance between graphene and hBN\ncrystals is the smallest among the three substrates, and that graphene-ammonia\ngas sensors based on a G/hBN heterostructure exhibit the fastest recovery times\nfor NH3 exposure and are slightly affected by wet or dry air environment.\nMoreover, the dependence of graphene-ammonia sensors on different substrates\nindicates that graphene sensors exhibit two different adsorption processes for\nNH3 molecules: one at the top of the graphene surface and another at its bottom\nside close to the substrate. Therefore, our findings show that substrate\nengineering is crucial to the development of graphene-based gas sensors and\nindicate additional routes for faster sensors.',

   'value': 'Mass measurement of graphene using quartz crystal microbalances'},
  'summary': 'Current wafer-scale fabrication methods for graphene-based electronics and\nsensors involve the transfer of single-layer graphene by a support polymer.\nThis often leaves some polymer residue on the graphene, which can strongly\nimpact its electronic, thermal, and mechanical resonance properties. To assess\nthe cleanliness of graphene fabrication methods, it is thus of considerable\ninterest to quantify the amount of contamination on top of the graphene. Here,\nwe present a methodology for direct measurement of the mass of the graphene\nsheet using quartz crystal microbalances (QCM). By monitoring the QCM resonance\nfrequency during removal of graphene in an oxygen plasma, the total mass of the\ngraphene and contamination is determined with sub-graphene-monolayer accuracy.\nSince the etch-rate of the contamination is higher than that of graphene,\nquantitative measurements of the mass of contaminants below, on top, and\nbetween graphene layers are obtained. We find that polymer-based dry transfer\nmethods can increase the mass of a graphene sheet by a factor of 10. The\npresented mass measurement method is conceptually straightforward to interpret\nand can be used for standardized testing of graphene transfer procedures in\norder to improve the quality of graphene devices in future applications.',

This can be explained by\nthe different density of states in mono-layer and bi-layer/tri-layer graphenes.\nIn mono-layer, the mobility also decreases with increasing temperature\nprimarily due to surface polar substrate phonon scattering. In particular, improvements in epitaxial graphene growth,\ninterface control and the understanding of multilayer epitaxial graphene's\nelectronic properties are discussed. Although graphene grown on both polar\nfaces of SiC is addressed, our discussions will focus on graphene grown on the\n(000-1) C-face of SiC. The unique properties of C-face multilayer epitaxial\ngraphene have become apparent. These films behave electronically like a stack\nof nearly independent graphene sheets rather than a thin Bernal-stacked\ngraphite sample. The origin of multilayer graphene's electronic behavior is its\nunique highly-ordered stacking of non-Bernal rotated graphene planes. While\nthese rotations do not significantly affect the inter-layer interactions, they\ndo break the stacking symmetry of graphite.   'summary': 'All carbon electronics based on graphene has been an elusive goal. For more\nthan a decade, the inability to produce significant band-gaps in this material\nhas prevented the development of semiconducting graphene. While chemical\nfunctionalization was thought to be a route to semiconducting graphene,\ndisorder in the chemical adsorbates, leading to low mobilities, have proved to\nbe a hurdle in its production.    'value': 'Metal/graphene interfaces generated by electrode deposition induce barriers\nor potential modulations influencing the electronic transport properties of\ngraphene based devices. However, their impact on the local mechanical\nproperties of graphene is much less studied. Here we show that graphene near a\nmetallic interface can exhibit a set of ripples self-organized in domains whose\ntopographic roughness is controlled by the tip bias of a scanning tunneling\nmicroscope. The reconstruction from topographic images of graphene bending\nenergy maps sheds light on the local electro-mechanical response of graphene\nunder STM imaging and unveils the role of the stress induced by the vicinity of\ngraphene/metal interface in the formation and the manipulation of these\nripples. Since microscopic rippling is one of the important factors that limit\ncharge carrier mobility in graphene, the control of rippling with a gate\nvoltage may have important consequences in the conductance of graphene devices\nwhere transverse electric fields are created by contactless suspended gate\nelectrodes. This opens also the possibility to dynamically control the local\nmorphology of graphene nanomembranes.'},

  'summary': 'We invented a method to fabricate graphene transistors on oxidized silicon\nwafers without the need to transfer graphene layers. To stimulate the growth of\ngraphene layers on oxidized silicon a catalyst system of nanometer thin\naluminum/nickel double layer is used. The\npaper examines the physical-chemical harmony and/or confrontation in terms of\nthe molecular theory of graphene. Chemistry that is consistent with graphene\nphysics expectations involves: small mass of carbon atoms, which provides a\nlightweight material; sp2 configuration of the atoms valence electrons,\nensuring a flat 2D structure of condensed benzenoid units; high strength of C-C\nvalence bonds responsible for exclusive mechanical strength. Chemistry that is\nin conflict with graphene physics expectations covers: radical character of\ngraphene material; collective character of electronic system of graphene,\npreventing from localization of its response on any external impact; the\nmolecular nature and topochemical character of graphene mechanics; the\nmolecular nature of graphene magnetism.   'summary': 'Since the advent of graphene, a variety of studies have been performed to\nelucidate its fundamental physics, or to explore its practical applications.\nGate-tunable resistance is one of the most important properties of graphene and\nhas been studied in 1-3 layer graphene in a number of efforts to control the\nband gap to obtain a large on-off ratio. On the other hand, the transport\nproperty of multilayer graphene with more than three layers is less well\nunderstood. Here we show a new aspect of multilayer graphene. We found that\nfour-layer graphene shows intrinsic peak structures in the gate voltage\ndependence of its resistance at zero magnetic field. Measurement of quantum\noscillations in magnetic field confirmed that the peaks originate from the\nspecific band structure of graphene and appear at the carrier density for the\nbottoms of conduction bands and valence bands. The intrinsic peak structures\nshould generally be observed in AB-stacked multilayer graphene. The present\nresults would be significant for understanding the physics of graphene and\nmaking graphene FET devices.',

  'summary': 'This article reviews the main theoretical and experimental advances regarding\nthe interaction between surface acoustic waves (SAWs) and electronic\nexcitations in graphene. The coupling of the graphene electron gas to the SAW\npiezoelectric field can modify the propagation properties of the SAW, and even\namplify the intensity of SAWs traveling along the graphene layer. Conversely,\nthe periodic electric and strain fields of the SAW can be used to modify the\ngraphene Dirac cone and to couple light into graphene plasmons. Finally, SAWs\ncan generate acousto-electric currents in graphene. These increase linearly\nwith the SAW frequency and power but, in contrast to conventional currents,\nthey depend non-monotonously on the graphene electric conductivity. Most of\nthese functionalities have been reported in graphene transferred to the surface\nof strong piezoelectric insulators. The recent observation of acousto-electric\ncurrents in epitaxial graphene on SiC opens the way to the large-scale\nfabrication of graphene-based acousto-electric devices patterned directly on a\nsemi-insulating wafer.',

  'summary': "A blueprint for producing scalable digital graphene electronics has remained\nelusive. Current methods to produce semiconducting-metallic graphene networks\nall suffer from either stringent lithographic demands that prevent\nreproducibility, process-induced disorder in the graphene, or scalability\nissues. Using angle resolved photoemission, we have discovered a unique one\ndimensional metallic-semiconducting-metallic junction made entirely from\ngraphene, and produced without chemical functionalization or finite size\npatterning. The junction is produced by taking advantage of the inherent,\natomically ordered, substrate-graphene interaction when it is grown on SiC, in\nthis case when graphene is forced to grow over patterned SiC steps. This\nscalable bottomup approach allows us to produce a semiconducting graphene strip\nwhose width is precisely defined within a few graphene lattice constants, a\nlevel of precision entirely outside modern lithographic limits. The\narchitecture demonstrated in this work is so robust that variations in the\naverage electronic band structure of thousands of these patterned ribbons have\nlittle variation over length scales tens of microns long. The semiconducting\ngraphene has a topologically defined few nanometer wide region with an energy\ngap greater than 0.5 eV in an otherwise continuous metallic graphene sheet.\nThis work demonstrates how the graphene-substrate interaction can be used as a\npowerful tool to scalably modify graphene's electronic structure and opens a\nnew direction in graphene electronics research.",

  'summary': 'The implementation of graphene in semiconducting technology requires the\nprecise knowledge about the graphene-semiconductor interface. In our work the\nstructure and electronic properties of the graphene/$n$-Ge(110) interface are\ninvestigated on the local (nm) and macro (from $\\mu\\mathrm{m}$ to mm) scales\nvia a combination of different microscopic and spectroscopic surface science\ntechniques accompanied by density functional theory calculations. The\nelectronic structure of freestanding graphene remains almost completely intact\nin this system, with only a moderate $n$-doping indicating weak interaction\nbetween graphene and the Ge substrate. With regard to the optimization of\ngraphene growth it is found that the substrate temperature is a crucial factor,\nwhich determines the graphene layer alignment on the Ge(110) substrate during\nits growth from the atomic carbon source. Moreover, our results demonstrate\nthat the preparation routine for graphene on the doped semiconducting material\n($n$-Ge) leads to the effective segregation of dopants at the interface between\ngraphene and Ge(110). Furthermore, it is shown that these dopant atoms might\nform regular structures at the graphene/Ge interface and induce the doping of\ngraphene. Our findings help to understand the interface properties of the\ngraphene-semiconductor interfaces and the effect of dopants on the electronic\nstructure of graphene in such systems.',

  'title_detail': {'type': 'text/plain',

   'value': 'Graphene Helicoid: The Distinct Properties Promote Application of\n  Graphene Related Materials in Thermal Management'},
  'summary': 'The extremely high thermal conductivity of graphene has received great\nattention both in experiments and calculations. Obviously, new feature in\nthermal properties is of primary importance for application of graphene-based\nmaterials in thermal management in nanoscale. Here, we studied the thermal\nconductivity of graphene helicoid, a newly reported graphene-related\nnanostructure, using molecular dynamics simulation. Interestingly, in contrast\nto the converged cross-plane thermal conductivity in multi-layer graphene,\naxial thermal conductivity of graphene helicoid keeps increasing with thickness\nwith a power law scaling relationship, which is a consequence of the divergent\nin-plane thermal conductivity of two-dimensional graphene. Moreover, the large\noverlap between adjacent layers in graphene helicoid also promotes higher\nthermal conductivity than multi-layer graphene. Furthermore, in the small\nstrain regime (< 10%), compressive strain can effectively increase the thermal\nconductivity of graphene helicoid, while in the ultra large strain regime\n(~100% to 500%), tensile strain does not decrease the heat current, unlike that\nin generic solid-state materials. Our results reveal that the divergence in\nthermal conductivity, associated with the anomalous strain dependence and the\nunique structural flexibility, make graphene helicoid a new platform for\nstudying fascinating phenomena of key relevance to the scientific understanding\nand technological applications of graphene-related materials.',

  'summary': "We present detailed Raman studies of graphene deposited on gallium nitride\nnanowires with different variations in height. Our results show that different\ndensity and height of nanowires being in contact with graphene impact graphene\nproperties like roughness, strain and carrier concentration as well as density\nand type of induced defects. Detailed analysis of Raman spectra of graphene\ndeposited on different nanowire substrates shows that bigger differences in\nnanowires height increase graphene strain, while higher number of nanowires in\ncontact with graphene locally reduce the strain. Moreover, the value of\ngraphene carrier concentration is found to be correlated with the density of\nnanowires in contact with graphene. Analysis of intensity ratios of Raman G, D\nand D' bands enable to trace how nanowire substrate impacts the defect\nconcentration and type. The lowest concentration of defects is observed for\ngraphene deposited on nanowires of the lowest density. Contact between graphene\nand densely arranged nanowires leads to a large density of vacancies. On the\nother hand, grain boundaries are the main type of defects in graphene on rarely\ndistributed nanowires. Our results also show modification of graphene carrier\nconcentration and strain by different types of defects present in graphene.",

  'title': 'The Inherent Behavior of Graphene Flakes in Water: A Molecular Dynamics\n  Study',
  'title_detail': {'type': 'text/plain',
   'language': None,
   'base': 'http://export.arxiv.org/api/query?search_query=graphene&id_list=&start=0&max_results=100&sortBy=relevance&sortOrder=descending',
   'value': 'The Inherent Behavior of Graphene Flakes in Water: A Molecular Dynamics\n  Study'},
  'summary': 'Graphene-water interaction has been under scrutiny ever since graphene\ndiscovery and realization of its exceptional properties. Several computational\nand experimental reports exist that have tried to look into the interactions\ninvolved, however, none of them addresses the issue in its entirety. We have\ntested the inherent hydrophobic behavior of a small graphene in water droplet\nby the means of MD simulations. The analysis has been extended to multiple\ngraphene flakes in water and their respective size dependent responses to water\ndroplet. Graphene retreats from water droplet to encapsulate it from the\nsurface. This response was highly dependent upon graphene size with respect to\nwater content. Additionally, we also report self-assembly of multilayered\ngraphene in water by means of MD simulations, an observation which can be\nutilized to synthesize such structures in a cost-effective way by\nexperimentalists. To fully comprehend graphene behavior in water, graphene\ndeformation was analyzed in the presence of water molecules. It was noticed\nthat graphene wrinkled to wrap around water molecules and resisted complete\nfailure, one that is seen in case of a sole graphene sheet. Our work will not\nonly address the question about whether graphene is hydrophobic or hydrophilic\nbut also provide insight into the behavior of graphene surface and mobility\nwhen exposed to water which can be exploited in numerous applications.',

Several computational\nand experimental reports exist that have tried to look into the interactions\ninvolved, however, none of them addresses the issue in its entirety. We have\ntested the inherent hydrophobic behavior of a small graphene in water droplet\nby the means of MD simulations. The analysis has been extended to multiple\ngraphene flakes in water and their respective size dependent responses to water\ndroplet. Graphene retreats from water droplet to encapsulate it from the\nsurface. This response was highly dependent upon graphene size with respect to\nwater content. Additionally, we also report self-assembly of multilayered\ngraphene in water by means of MD simulations, an observation which can be\nutilized to synthesize such structures in a cost-effective way by\nexperimentalists. To fully comprehend graphene behavior in water, graphene\ndeformation was analyzed in the presence of water molecules. It was noticed\nthat graphene wrinkled to wrap around water molecules and resisted complete\nfailure, one that is seen in case of a sole graphene sheet. Our work will not\nonly address the question about whether graphene is hydrophobic or hydrophilic\nbut also provide insight into the behavior of graphene surface and mobility\nwhen exposed to water which can be exploited in numerous applications.'},

   'value': 'Morphology of Ti on Monolayer Nanocrystalline Graphene and its\n  Unexpectedly Low Hydrogen Adsorption'},
  'summary': 'Hydrogen adsorption on graphene can be increased by functionalization with\nTi. This requires dispersing Ti islands on graphene as small and dense as\npossible, in order to increase the number of hydrogen adsorption sites per Ti\natom. In this report, we investigate the morphology of Ti on nanocrystalline\ngraphene and its hydrogen adsorption by scanning tunneling microscopy and\nthermal desorption spectroscopy, and compare the results with equivalent\nmeasurements on single-crystalline graphene. Nanocrystalline graphene consists\nof extremely small crystal grains of < 5 nm size. Ti atoms preferentially\nadsorb at the grain boundaries of nanocrystalline graphene and form smaller and\ndenser islands compared to single-crystalline graphene. Surprisingly, however,\nhydrogen adsorbs less to Ti on nanocrystalline graphene than to Ti on\nsingle-crystalline graphene. In particular, hydrogen hardly chemisorbs to 1 ML\nof Ti on nanocrystalline graphene. This may be attributed to strong bonds\nbetween Ti and defects located along the grain boundaries in nanocrystalline\ngraphene. This mechanism might apply to other metals, as well, and therefore\nour results suggest that when functionalizing graphene by metal atoms for the\npurpose of hydrogen storage or other chemical reactions, it is important to\nconsider not only the morphology of the resulting surface, but also the\ninfluence of graphene on the electronic states of the metal.',

   'value': 'Reversible doping of graphene field effect transistors by molecular\n  hydrogen: the role of the metal/graphene interface'},
  'summary': 'In this work, we present an investigation regarding how and why molecular\nhydrogen changes the electronic properties of graphene field effect\ntransistors. We demonstrate that interaction with H2 leads to local doping of\ngraphene near of the graphene-contact heterojunction. We also show that such\ninteraction is strongly dependent on the characteristics of the metal-graphene\ninterface. By changing the type metal in the contact, we observe that Ohmic\ncontacts can be strongly or weakly electrostatically coupled with graphene. For\nstrongly coupled contacts, the signature of the charge transfer effect promoted\nby the contacts results on an asymmetric ambipolar conduction, and such\nasymmetry can be tunable under interaction with H2. On the other hand, for\ncontacts weakly coupled with graphene, the hydrogen interaction has a more\nprofound effect. In such situation, the devices show a second charge neutrality\npoint in graphene transistor transfer curves (a double-peak response) upon H2\nexposure. We propose that this double-peak phenomenon arises from the\ndecoupling of the work function of graphene and that of the metallic electrodes\ninduced by the H2 molecules. We also show that the gas-induced modifications at\nthe metal-graphene interface can be exploited to create a controlled graphene\np-n junction, with considerable electron transfer to graphene layer and\nsignificant variation in the graphene resistance. These effects can pave the\nway for a suitable metallic contact engineering providing great potential for\nthe application of such devices as gas sensors.',

  'summary': 'Two experimental studies reported the spontaneous formation of amorphous and\ncrystalline structures of C60 intercalated between graphene and a substrate.\nThey observed interesting phenomena ranging from reaction between C60 molecules\nunder graphene to graphene sagging between the molecules and control of strain\nin graphene. Motivated by these works, we performed fully atomistic reactive\nmolecular dynamics simulations to study the formation and thermal stability of\ngraphene wrinkles as well as graphene attachment to and detachment from the\nsubstrate when graphene is laid over a previously distributed array of C60\nmolecules on a copper substrate at different values of temperature. As graphene\ncompresses the C60 molecules against the substrate, and graphene attachment to\nthe substrate between C60s ("C60s" stands for plural of C60) depends on the\nheight of graphene wrinkles, configurations with both frozen and non-frozen\nC60s structures were investigated in order to verify the experimental result of\nstable sagged graphene when the distance between C60s is about 4 nm and height\nof graphene wrinkles is about 0.8 nm. Below the distance of 4 nm between C60s,\ngraphene becomes locally suspended and less strained. We show that this happens\nwhen C60s are allowed to deform under the compressive action of graphene. If we\nkeep the C60s frozen, spontaneous "blanketing" of graphene happens only when\nthe distance between them are equal or above 7 nm. Both above results for the\nexistence of stable sagged graphene for C60 distances of 4 or 7 nm are shown to\nagree with a mechanical model relating the rigidity of graphene to the energy\nof graphene-substrate adhesion. In particular, this study might help the\ndevelopment of 2D confined nanoreactors that are considered in literature to be\nthe next advanced step on chemical reactions.',

  'summary': 'The synthesis of single-wall carbon nanotubes (SWCNTs) of desired diameters\nand chiralities is critical to the design of nanoscale electronic devices with\ndesired properties.1-6 The existing methods are based on self-assembly, 7-16\ntherefore lacking the control over their diameters and chiralities. The present\nwork reports a direct route to roll graphene. Specifically, we found that the\nelectrostatic deposition of graphene yielded: (i) flat graphene layers under\nhigh vacuum (10-7 Torr), (ii) completely scrolled graphene under hydrogen\natmosphere, (iii) partially scrolled graphene under nitrogen atmosphere, and\n(iv) no scrolling for helium atmospheres. Our study shows that the application\nof the electrostatic field facilitates the rolling of graphene sheets exposed\nto appropriate gases and allows the rolling of any size graphene. The technique\nproposed here, in conjunction with a technique that produces graphene\nnanoribbons (GNRs) of uniform widths, will have significant impact on the\ndevelopment of carbon nanotube based devices. Furthermore, the present\ntechnique may be applied to obtain tubes/scrolls of other layered materials.',

   'value': 'Interface states and anomalous quantum oscillations in graphene hybrid\n  structures'},
  'summary': 'One- and two-layer graphene have recently been shown to feature new physical\nphenomena such as unconventional quantum Hall effects and prospects of\nsupporting a non-silicon technological platform using epitaxial graphene. While\nboth one- and two-layer graphene have been studied extensively, continuous\nsheets of graphene possessing both parts have not yet been explored. Here we\nreport a study of such graphene hybrid structures. In a bulk hybrid featuring\ntwo large-area one- and two-layer graphene and an interface between them, two\nsets of Landau levels and features related to the interface were found. In edge\nhybrids featuring a large two-layer graphene with narrow one-layer graphene\nedges, we observed an anomalous suppression in quantum oscillation amplitude\ndue to the locking of one- and two-layer graphene Fermi energies and emergent\nchiral interface states. These findings demonstrate the importance of these\nhybrid structures whose unique interface states and related phenomena deserve\nfurther studies.',

  'summary': 'Graphene has many unique properties that make it an ideal material for\nfundamental studies as well as for potential applications. Here we review the\nrecent results on the Raman spectroscopy and imaging of graphene. Raman\nspectroscopy and imaging can be used as a quick and unambiguous method to\ndetermine the number of graphene layers. Following, the strong Raman signal of\nsingle layer graphene compared to graphite is explained by an interference\nenhancement model. We have also studied the effect of substrates, the top layer\ndeposition, the annealing process, as well as folding (stacking order) on the\nphysical and electronic properties of graphene. Finally, Raman spectroscopy of\nepitaxial graphene grown on SiC substrate is presented and strong compressive\nstrain on epitaxial graphene is observed.    'value': 'Second-order Overtone and Combinational Raman Modes of Graphene Layers\n  in the Range of 1690 cm-1 to 2150 cm-1'},
  'summary': 'Though graphene has been intensively studied by Raman spectroscopy, in this\nletter, we report a study of second-order overtone and combinational Raman\nmodes in an unexplored range of 1690-2150 cm-1 in nonsuspended commensurate\n(AB-stacked), incommensurate (folded) and suspended graphene layers. Based on\nthe double resonance theory, four dominant modes in this range have been\nassigned as 2oTO (M band), iTA+LO, iTO+LA and LO+LA. Differing to AB-stacked\nbilayer graphene or few layer graphene, the M band disappears in single layer\ngraphene. Systematic analysis reveals that interlayer interaction is essential\nfor the presence (or absence) of M band whereas the substrate has no effect on\nthis. Dispersive behaviors of these "new" Raman modes in graphene have been\nprobed by the excitation energy dependent Raman spectroscopy. It is found that\nthe appearance of the M band strictly relies on the AB stacking, which could be\na fingerprint of AB-stacked bilayer graphene. This work expands the unique and\npowerful abilities of Raman spectroscopy on study of graphene and provides\nanother effective way to probe phonon dispersion, electron-phonon coupling, and\nto exploit electronic band structure of graphene layers.',

  'summary': 'Controlled modification of graphene properties is essential for its proposed\nelectronic applications. Here we describe a possibility of tuning electrical\nproperties of graphene via electron beam irradiation. We show that by\ncontrolling the irradiation dose one can change the carrier mobility and\nincrease the resistance at the minimum conduction point in the single layer\ngraphene. The bilayer graphene is less susceptible to the electron beam\nirradiation. The modification of graphene properties via irradiation can be\nmonitored and quantified by the changes in the disorder D peak in Raman\nspectrum of graphene. The obtained results may lead to a new method of defect\nengineering of graphene physical properties, and to the procedure of "writing"\ngraphene circuits via e-beam irradiation. The results also have implications\nfor fabrication of graphene nanodevices, which involve scanning electron\nmicroscopy and electron beam lithography.',

  'summary': 'Controlling the type and density of charge carriers by doping is the key step\nfor developing graphene electronics. However, direct doping of graphene is\nrather a challenge. Based on first-principles calculations, a concept of\novercoming doping difficulty in graphene via substrate is reported.We find that\ndoping could be strongly enhanced in epitaxial graphene grown on silicon\ncarbide substrate. Compared to free-standing graphene, the formation energies\nof the dopants can decrease by as much as 8 eV. The type and density of the\ncharge carriers of epitaxial graphene layer can be effectively manipulated by\nsuitable dopants and surface passivation. More importantly, contrasting to the\ndirect doping of graphene, the charge carriers in epitaxial graphene layer are\nweakly scattered by dopants due to the spatial separation between dopants and\nthe conducting channel. Finally, we show that a similar idea can also be used\nto control magnetic properties, for example, induce a half-metallic state in\nthe epitaxial graphene without magnetic impurity doping.',

  'summary': 'Graphene is intrinsically non-flat and corrugates randomly. Since the\ncorrugating physics of atomically-thin graphene is strongly tied to its\nelectronics properties, randomly corrugating morphology of graphene poses\nsignificant challenge to its application in nanoelectronic devices for which\nprecise (digital) control is the key.   'summary': 'Motivated by the recent photochlorination experiment [B. Li et al., ACS Nano\n5, 5957 (2011)], we study theoretically the interaction of chlorine with\ngraphene. In previous theoretical studies, covalent binding between chlorine\nand carbon atoms has been elusive upon adsorption to the graphene basal plane.\nInterestingly, in their recent experiment, Li et al. interpreted their data in\nterms of chemical bonding of chlorine on top of the graphene plane, associated\nwith a change from sp2 to sp3 in carbon hybridization and formation of graphene\nnanodomains. We study the hypothesis that these domains are actually fractured\ngraphene with chlorinated edges, and compare the energetics of\nchlorine-containing graphene edge terminations, both in zigzag and armchair\ndirections, to chlorine adsorption onto infinite graphene. Our results indicate\nthat edge chlorination is favored over adsorption in the experimental\nconditions with radical atomic chlorine and that edge chlorination with\nsp3-hybridized edge carbons is stable also in ambient conditions. An ab initio\nthermodynamical analysis shows that the presence of chlorine is able to break\nthe pristine graphene layer. Finally, we discuss the possible effects of the\nsilicon dioxide substrate on the chlorination of graphene.',

  'summary': 'We have studied the effect of photoelectrons on defect formation in graphene\nduring extreme ultraviolet (EUV) irradiation. Assuming the major role of these\nlow energy electrons, we have mimicked the process by using low energy primary\nelectrons. Graphene is irradiated by an electron beam with energy lower than 80\neV. After e-beam irradiation, it is found that the D peak, I(D), appears in the\nRaman spectrum, indicating defect formation in graphene. The evolution of\nI(D)/I(G) follows the amorphization trajectory with increasing irradiation\ndose, indicating that graphene goes through a transformation from\nmicrocrystalline to nanocrystalline and then further to amorphous carbon.\nFurther, irradiation of graphene with increased water partial pressure does not\nsignificantly change the Raman spectra, which suggests that, in the extremely\nlow energy range, e-beam induced chemical reactions between residual water and\ngraphene is not the dominant mechanism driving defect formation in graphene.\nSingle layer graphene, partially suspended over holes was irradiated with EUV\nradiation. By comparing with the Raman results from e-beam irradiation, it is\nconcluded that the photoelectrons, especially those from the valence band,\ncontribute to defect formation in graphene during irradiation.',

  'summary': 'Large area graphene sheets grown by chemical vapor deposition can potentially\nbe employed as a transparent electrode in photovoltaics if their sheet\nresistance can be significantly lowered, without any loss in transparency.\nHere, we report the fabrication of a graphene-conducting-carbon-nanotube (CCNT)\nhybrid material with a sheet resistance considerably lower than neat graphene,\nand with the requisite small reduction in transparency. Graphene is deposited\non top of a a self-assembled CCNT monolayer which creates parallel conducting\npaths on the graphene surface. The hybrid thereby circumvents electron\nscattering due to defects in the graphene sheet, and reduces the sheet\nresistance by a factor of two. The resistance can be further reduced by\nchemically doping the hybrid. Moreover, the chemically doped hybrid is more\nstable than a standalone chemically doped graphene sheet, as the CCNT network\nenhances the dopant binding. In order to understand the results, we develop a\n2D resistance network model in which we couple the CCNT layer to the graphene\nsheet and demonstrate the model accounts quantitatively for the resistance\ndecrease. Our results show that a graphene-CCNT hybrid system has high\npotential for use as a transparent electrode with high transparency and low\nsheet resistance.',

   'value': 'Homogeneous bilayer graphene film based flexible transparent conductor'},
  'summary': 'Graphene is considered a promising candidate to replace conventional\ntransparent conductors due to its low opacity, high carrier mobility and\nflexible structure. Multi-layer graphene or stacked single layer graphenes have\nbeen investigated in the past but both have their drawbacks. The uniformity of\nmulti-layer graphene is still questionable, and single layer graphene stacks\nrequire many transfer processes to achieve sufficiently low sheet resistance.\nIn this work, bilayer graphene film grown with low pressure chemical vapor\ndeposition was used as a transparent conductor for the first time. The\ntechnique was demonstrated to be highly efficient in fabricating a conductive\nand uniform transparent conductor compared to multi-layer or single layer\ngraphene. Four transfers of bilayer graphene yielded a transparent conducting\nfilm with a sheet resistance of 180 {\\Omega}_{\\square} at a transmittance of\n83%. Do another query for the topic “quantum dots”, while noting how you can manipulate results if you desire.


quantum_dots = arxiv.query(
  query="quantum dots",
  id_list=[],
  max_results=None,
  start = 0,
  sort_by="relevance",
  sort_order="descending",
  prune=True,
  iterative=False,
  max_chunk_results=1000
)

Organize and analyze your results¶

Create a dataframe to better analyze your results.

qd_df = pd.DataFrame(quantum_dots)

We’ll look at the first 10 results.

Visualize your results¶

Get a sense of the how your topic has trended over time. When did research on your topic take off? Assign datetime functionality to the column “published” which lists when the article was published and then create a bar chart.

qd_df["published"] = qd_df["published"].astype("datetime64")

qd_df["published"].groupby(qd_df["published"].dt.year).count().plot(kind="bar")

<AxesSubplot:xlabel='published'>

Explore authors to see who is publishing your topic. Group by author, then sort and select the top 20 authors.

qd_authors = qd_df.groupby(qd_df["author"])["author"].count().sort_values(ascending=False)

Identify and download papers¶
